- [07:33] modern trait personality researchers believe that you can assess personality
- [07:36] traits by having people answer a series of test questions there are lots of so
- [07:40] called personality trait inventories out there some provide a quick reading on a
- [07:44] particular enduring trait like anxiety or self-esteem
- [07:47] while others gauge a wide range of traits like our friends the Big Five
- [07:51] these tests like the myers-briggs which you might have heard of involve long
- [07:54] questionnaires of true false or agree/disagree questions like do you
- [07:58] enjoy being the center of attention do you find it easy to empathize with
- [08:02] others or do you value justice over mercy but the classic Minnesota
- [08:06] multiphasic personality inventory is probably the most widely used
- [08:10] personality test the most recent version asks a series of five
- [08:14] in 67 true/false questions varying from no one seems to understand me too I like
- [08:19] mechanics magazines too I loved my father and is often used to identify
- [08:23] emotional disorders and there's how bandura's social cognitive camp sizes
- [08:27] you up because this school of thought emphasizes the interaction of
- [08:30] environment and behavior rather than just traits alone
- [08:33] they aren't solely into questions and answers instead they might measure
- [08:36] personality in different contexts understanding behavior in one situation
- [08:40] is best predicted by how you acted in a similar situation like if Bernice
- [08:44] freaked out and tried to hide under the bed during the last five thunderstorms
- [08:48] we can predict the chip will do that again next time and if we conducted a
- [08:51] controlled lab experiment where say we looked at the effects of thunderstorm
- [08:54] noises on people's behavior we might get an even better sense of what baseline
- [08:58] psychological factors could best predict storm induced freak-outs and finally
- [09:02] there are the humanistic theorists like Maslow they often reject standardized
- [09:06] assessments altogether instead they tend to measure your self-concept through
- [09:10] therapy interviews and questionnaires that asked subjects to describe both how
- [09:14] they would ideally like to be and how they actually are the idea is that the
- [09:18] closer the actual and ideal are the more positive the subjects sense of self
- [09:22] which brings us back to that biggest motherlode question of them all who or
- [09:26] what is the self all the books out there about self esteem self-help self
- [09:31] awareness self control and so on are built upon one assumption that the self
- [09:35] is the organizer of our thoughts and feelings and actions essentially the
- [09:39] center of a personality but of course it's a sticky issue one way to think
- [09:43] about self is through the concept of possible selves like your ideal self
- [09:47] perhaps devastatingly attractive and intelligent successful and well loved as
- [09:51] well as your most feared self the one who could end up unemployed and lonely
- [09:54] and run down this balance of potential best and worst selves motivates us
- [09:58] through life. In the end, once you factor in environment and childhood experiences,
- [10:02] culture, and all that mess, not to mention biology, which we haven't even touched on
- [10:06] today, can we really firmly define self or
- [10:09] answer certainly that we even have one? That, my friend, is one of life's biggest
- [10:13] questions, insofar as it has yet to be universally answered. But you learned a lot,
